<ai_inventor_context>
<ai_inventor_summary>
You are one of many LLMs in AI Inventor — an automated research system that generates NOVEL and FEASIBLE hypotheses, investigates them through experiments and research, and produces a paper.

</ai_inventor_summary>

<your_role>
YOU ARE: An artifact executor (Step 3.3: GEN_ART in the invention loop)

Executing a plan to produce a concrete artifact.
GEN_PAPER_TEXT will use your artifact in the next paper draft.

Rigorous artifact with clear results → strong paper. Sloppy artifact → misdirected research.
</your_role>
</ai_inventor_context>

<task>
Conduct thorough, unbiased research on the given topic.
Adapt your investigation approach based on the research question and domain.
</task>

<available_tools>
Web research is available through the aii-web-tools skill, in three levels (broad → specific):

1. web search — Returns titles, URLs, snippets. Use first to discover and scan the landscape.
2. web fetch — Reads a page and returns its content as markdown (HTML or PDF). Use to understand a source. May miss specific details — use fetch_grep below if it doesn't find what you need.
3. fetch_grep — Regex search over a page/PDF's full text. Returns exact matching sections with context. Use for precise details, exact numbers, methodology, or PDFs.

Workflow: search → fetch (understand) → fetch_grep (extract specifics).
</available_tools>

<tool_use>
Maximize parallel tool calls. Parallelize independent operations, only sequentialize dependencies.
- Multiple searches/fetches on different topics → parallel in one turn
- Search then fetch results → sequential (need URLs first)
</tool_use>

<critical_requirements>
1. SOURCE DIVERSITY - Consult MANY sources (10+), not just the first few results
2. AVOID SELECTION BIAS - Actively seek contradicting viewpoints, not just confirming ones
3. TRIANGULATE - Cross-reference claims across multiple independent sources
4. ACKNOWLEDGE UNCERTAINTY - Be honest about confidence levels and limitations
5. SYNTHESIZE - Produce a coherent answer that accounts for conflicting evidence
</critical_requirements>

<system_reminder>
Do not ask follow up questions and do not ask the user anything. Execute all steps independently.
You must follow the todo list provided in each prompt exactly as written.
No placeholders, stubs, or incomplete code — all code must be complete and functional.
</system_reminder>

<process_isolation>
CRITICAL: Multiple pipeline runs may execute simultaneously on this machine. `ps aux | grep method.py` matches ALL runs, not just yours.
- NEVER kill processes by name (`killall`, `pkill -f`, `ps aux | grep ... | xargs kill`). This kills OTHER runs' processes.
- NEVER monitor processes by name (`ps aux | grep method.py`). You will see other runs' processes and get confused.
- ALWAYS use PID-based process management:
  Run: `uv run method.py & PID=$!` or `timeout <seconds> uv run method.py & PID=$!`
  Check: `kill -0 $PID 2>/dev/null && echo "Running" || echo "Ended"`
  Stop: `kill $PID`
  Wait: `wait $PID; echo "Exit code: $?"`
  Monitor: `tail -f logs/run.log & TAIL_PID=$!` then `kill $TAIL_PID` when done
</process_isolation>
```

<artifact_plan>
id: gen_plan_research_1_idx1
type: research
title: Rateless MinHash Theory Foundations Research
summary: >-
  Survey MinHash variants, fountain codes, and rate-distortion theory to establish theoretical foundations for rateless MinHash
  design
runpod_compute_profile: cpu_light
question: >-
  How can fountain code principles and rate-distortion theory be applied to create a rateless MinHash sketch that enables
  progressive Jaccard similarity estimation with optimal space-accuracy trade-offs?
research_plan: "## Phase 1: MinHash and Variants - Sketch Size Selection Problem (Priority: HIGH)\n\n### 1.1 Standard MinHash\
  \ Analysis\n- **Search**: 'MinHash Broder 1997 2000 Jaccard similarity estimation variance'\n- **Fetch**: Original MinHash\
  \ papers (Broder et al. STOC 1997, WWW 2000)\n- **Grep for**: Variance bounds, sketch size formulas, estimation error equations\n\
  - **Key questions**: \n  - What is the exact variance of MinHash estimator with k hashes?\n  - How is sketch size typically\
  \ selected in practice?\n  - What are the theoretical lower/upper bounds on required sketch size?\n\n### 1.2 b-bit MinHash\
  \ (Li & König)\n- **Search**: 'b-bit MinHash Li Konig 2011 2012 sketch size compression'\n- **Fetch**: Original b-bit MinHash\
  \ paper (WWW 2011 or similar)\n- **Grep for**: Sketch size formulas, bits per hash, trade-off analysis\n- **Key questions**:\n\
  \  - How does b-bit MinHash decide the optimal b?\n  - What is the space-accuracy trade-off curve?\n  - Can b be adapted\
  \ after sketch creation?\n\n### 1.3 SetSketch (Ertl)\n- **Search**: 'SetSketch Ertl 2017 2018 MinHash HyperLogLog hybrid'\n\
  - **Fetch**: SetSketch paper from arXiv or conference\n- **Grep for**: Parameter selection, configurable parameters, union\
  \ estimator\n- **Key questions**:\n  - What parameter must be fixed upfront in SetSketch?\n  - How does it compare to MinHash\
  \ in terms of adaptivity?\n  - Is the sketch size fixed at creation?\n\n### 1.4 Odd Sketch (Mitzenmacher et al.)\n- **Search**:\
  \ 'Odd Sketch Mitzenmacher 2020 2021 XOR Jaccard high similarity'\n- **Fetch**: Odd Sketch paper\n- **Grep for**: Sketch\
  \ structure, binary sketch, high similarity focus\n- **Key questions**:\n  - How is the sketch size determined?\n  - Does\
  \ it support progressive refinement?\n  - What are its limitations for general Jaccard values?\n\n### 1.5 ProbMinHash (Ertl)\n\
  - **Search**: 'ProbMinHash Ertl weighted Jaccard probability'\n- **Fetch**: ProbMinHash paper\n- **Grep for**: Algorithm\
  \ description, hash value generation\n- **Key questions**:\n  - How does it generate hash values?\n  - Is the sequence of\
  \ hashes finite or infinite?\n  - Could it be made rateless?\n\n## Phase 2: Fountain Codes - Rateless Encoding/Decoding\
  \ (Priority: HIGH)\n\n### 2.1 LT Codes (Luby Transform)\n- **Search**: 'LT codes Luby 2002 fountain codes rateless erasure'\n\
  - **Fetch**: Original LT codes paper (FOCS 2002 or similar)\n- **Grep for**: Encoding process, degree distribution, decoding\
  \ algorithm\n- **Key questions**:\n  - How are encoded symbols generated from source blocks?\n  - What is the degree distribution\
  \ (Robust Soliton)?\n  - How does decoding work (belief propagation)?\n  - What guarantees exist on recovery with n+k symbols?\n\
  \n### 2.2 Raptor Codes\n- **Search**: 'Raptor codes Shokrollahi 2006 fountain codes efficient'\n- **Fetch**: Raptor codes\
  \ paper (IEEE Transactions on Information Theory)\n- **Grep for**: Pre-coding, Luby Transform combination, linear time encoding/decoding\n\
  - **Key questions**:\n  - How do Raptor codes improve upon LT codes?\n  - What is the encoding/decoding complexity?\n  -\
  \ How are source symbols preprocessed?\n\n### 2.3 Digital Fountain Applications to Hashing\n- **Search**: 'fountain codes\
  \ hashing similarity search locality sensitive'\n- **Fetch**: Any papers applying fountain codes to LSH or hashing\n- **Key\
  \ questions**:\n  - Has anyone applied fountain codes to hashing before?\n  - What analogies exist between erasure codes\
  \ and sketching?\n  - Could MinHash be viewed as an erasure code?\n\n## Phase 3: Rate-Distortion Theory for Similarity Estimation\
  \ (Priority: HIGH)\n\n### 3.1 Rate-Distortion Function Basics\n- **Search**: 'rate-distortion theory Shannon 1948 MSE distortion\
  \ source coding'\n- **Fetch**: Coverage of rate-distortion theory (lecture notes, textbook chapters)\n- **Grep for**: R(D)\
  \ formula, reverse water-filling, Gaussian source\n- **Key questions**:\n  - What is the rate-distortion function for a\
  \ Bernoulli source?\n  - How does R(D) relate to sketch size and estimation error?\n  - Can we compute R(D) for Jaccard\
  \ similarity estimation?\n\n### 3.2 Rate-Distortion for Set Similarity\n- **Search**: 'rate-distortion set similarity MinHash\
  \ information theory'\n- **Fetch**: Any papers connecting rate-distortion to sketching or similarity estimation\n- **Key\
  \ questions**:\n  - Is there prior work on rate-distortion bounds for Jaccard estimation?\n  - What is the rate (bits) needed\
  \ to estimate Jaccard within MSE D?\n  - How does the set size affect the rate-distortion function?\n\n### 3.3 Optimal Stopping\
  \ Rules\n- **Search**: 'optimal stopping sequential estimation hypothesis testing'\n- **Fetch**: Papers on sequential estimation,\
  \ optimal stopping\n- **Grep for**: Stopping rules, sequential probability ratio test (SPRT), error bounds\n- **Key questions**:\n\
  \  - How to determine when to stop collecting hash values?\n  - What are sequential estimation techniques?\n  - Can rate-distortion\
  \ theory provide a stopping rule?\n\n## Phase 4: Progressive Estimation in LSH (Priority: MEDIUM)\n\n### 4.1 Adaptive LSH\
  \ Schemes\n- **Search**: 'adaptive LSH locality sensitive hashing progressive query'\n- **Fetch**: Papers on adaptive or\
  \ multi-probe LSH\n- **Grep for**: Progressive query processing, adaptive hash function selection\n- **Key questions**:\n\
  \  - Do any LSH schemes support progressive similarity estimation?\n  - How do multi-probe LSH or adaptive LSH work?\n \
  \ - Can we learn from their approach?\n\n### 4.2 Sketching with Progressive Refinement\n- **Search**: 'progressive sketching\
  \ streaming data approximate query'\n- **Fetch**: Papers on progressive sketching or refinement\n- **Grep for**: Refinement\
  \ process, error bounds that improve with more data\n- **Key questions**:\n  - Are there sketching algorithms that support\
  \ progressive refinement?\n  - How do they guarantee monotonic error improvement?\n  - What analogies can we draw to our\
  \ problem?\n\n## Phase 5: Synthesis and Gap Identification (Priority: HIGH)\n\n### 5.1 Gap Analysis\n- Synthesize findings\
  \ from Phases 1-4\n- Identify specific gaps our rateless MinHash can fill\n- **Key questions**:\n  - What is the exact problem\
  \ with fixed sketch size in existing MinHash variants?\n  - How can fountain code principles address this problem?\n  -\
  \ What rate-distortion bound should our scheme approach?\n\n### 5.2 Mathematical Framework Draft\n- Based on research, draft\
  \ a mathematical framework for rateless MinHash\n- **Components to outline**:\n  - Encoding: How to generate infinite hash\
  \ sequence from a set\n  - Decoding: How to estimate Jaccard from partial hash sequences\n  - Degree distribution: What\
  \ distribution over set elements for coding\n  - Stopping rule: How to decide enough hashes have been processed\n\n### 5.3\
  \ Related Work Table\n- Create a comparative table of MinHash variants\n- Columns: Sketch size fixed? Progressive? Space-efficient?\
  \ Handles diverse similarities?\n- Identify where rateless MinHash would fit\n\n## Execution Notes for Research Executor\n\
  \n1. **Time Allocation**: \n   - Phase 1: 45 mins (most critical for understanding current limitations)\n   - Phase 2: 45\
  \ mins (understand fountain code mechanics)\n   - Phase 3: 60 mins (most theoretical, rate-distortion derivation)\n   -\
  \ Phase 4: 30 mins (identify related progressive approaches)\n   - Phase 5: 30 mins (synthesis)\n\n2. **Search Strategy**:\n\
  \   - Start with broad searches to find survey papers or tutorials\n   - Follow citations backward (check references of\
  \ good papers)\n   - Check authors' homepages for tech reports or extended versions\n   - Use arXiv, Google Scholar, and\
  \ ACM DL via web search\n\n3. **Fetch Priority**:\n   - Fetch abstracts first to filter relevance\n   - Fetch full PDFs\
  \ only for highly relevant papers\n   - Use grep to extract specific equations, theorems, or experimental results\n\n4.\
  \ **Output Structure**:\n   - `research_report.md` should have sections matching the phases above\n   - `research_out.json`\
  \ should have:\n     - `answer`: Synthesized findings with clear gaps identified\n     - `sources`: All papers/urls with\
  \ bibtex citations if possible\n     - `follow_up_questions`: Specific questions for next research iteration\n\n5. **Failure\
  \ Scenarios**:\n   - If rate-distortion for Jaccard can't be found, derive it from first principles using Bernoulli process\
  \ model\n   - If fountain code application to hashing isn't found, analyze analogy between erasure channel and MinHash channel\n\
  \   - If progressive LSH isn't found, look broader at adaptive data structures or streaming algorithms\n\n6. **Tools Usage**:\n\
  \   - Use `aii_fast_web_search.py` for initial discovery (broad queries)\n   - Use `aii_fast_web_fetch.py fetch` for reading\
  \ paper abstracts and intros\n   - Use `aii_fast_web_fetch.py grep` for extracting specific formulas, theorems, or experimental\
  \ numbers from PDFs\n   - Parallelize independent searches (different topics) but sequence fetch after search\n\n7. **Specific\
  \ URLs to try**:\n   - arXiv.org search for MinHash variants\n   - scholar.google.com for citation tracking\n   - DOI.org\
  \ for accessing specific papers if DOIs are known\n   - Authors' homepages (e.g., Ping Li, Mario Ertl, Michael Mitzenmacher)\n\
  \n8. **Deliverables check**:\n   - [ ] Comprehensive comparison of MinHash variants with their sketch size limitations\n\
  \   - [ ] Explanation of fountain code principles with mathematical notation\n   - [ ] Rate-distortion function for Jaccard\
  \ estimation (derived or cited)\n   - [ ] Identification of at least 2 specific gaps our approach can fill\n   - [ ] Preliminary\
  \ mathematical framework for rateless MinHash\n   - [ ] Bibliography with 15-20 relevant sources"
explanation: >-
  This research is critical because: (1) We need to precisely understand the sketch size selection problem in existing MinHash
  variants to motivate our rateless approach; (2) Fountain code principles must be understood in enough detail to apply them
  to hash generation; (3) Rate-distortion theory can provide the optimal bound our scheme should approach, and may yield a
  computable stopping rule; (4) Understanding progressive estimation in related fields can inform our algorithm design. Without
  this foundational research, we risk designing a rateless MinHash that doesn't actually address the right problem or achieve
  optimal performance. The executor needs specific search queries, URLs to try, and clear questions to answer to produce a
  research report that directly informs our hypothesis testing.
</artifact_plan>

<investigation_process>
1. DIVERGE: Brainstorm multiple angles/framings of the question before searching. Think across fields — what adjacent domains might have relevant insights?
2. SEARCH: Multiple queries per angle with different phrasings to discover the landscape
3. FETCH: Read promising URLs at high level. Snippets are NOT enough — fetch full pages
4. DETAIL: aii-web-tools fetch_grep for specifics from key pages/PDFs
5. CONTRAST: Actively try to disprove your emerging conclusions. Search with different phrasings, "[topic] criticism", "[topic] limitations". Check across fields — the same finding may exist under different names
6. SYNTHESIZE: Integrate into balanced conclusion
7. ITERATE: Expect to repeat steps 2-6 if findings are incomplete or one-sided. Don't settle on first results
8. SUMMARIZE: Output JSON must include 'title' and 'summary' fields
</investigation_process>

<output_requirements>
- Write research_out.json to your workspace with all findings
- Provide your finding as clear prose WITH NUMBERED CITATIONS
- EVERY factual claim must have a citation number in brackets: [1], [2], [1, 3], etc.
- Include BOTH supporting AND contradicting evidence
- Be explicit about confidence level and what would change it
- End with follow-up questions for further investigation
</output_requirements>

{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Web tools\n\nYou have three web capabilities: **search**, **fetch**, and **grep** (exact\nregex extraction over a full page or PDF).\n\n**Pick where they come from, in this order:**\n\n1. **If you have built-in `WebSearch` / `WebFetch` tools, PREFER those over the\n   scripts below.** They may be **deferred tools** (listed by name but with\n   schemas not yet loaded) \u2014 if so, call `ToolSearch(\"select:WebSearch,WebFetch\")`\n   ONCE to load them, then use them normally. version](/w/index.php?title=MinHash&printable=yes \"Printable version of this page \\[p\\]\")\n\n\n\nIn other projects\n\n  * [Wikidata item](https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Special:EntityPage/Q11091745 \"Structured data on this page hosted by Wikidata \\[g\\]\")\n\n\n\nAppearance\n\nmove to sidebar hide\n\nFrom Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia\n\nData mining technique\n\nIn [computer science](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science \"Computer science\") and [data mining](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_mining \"Data mining\"), **MinHash** (or the **min-wise independent permutations** [locality sensitive hashing](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locality_sensitive_hashing \"Locality sensitive hashing\") scheme) is a technique for quickly estimating how [similar](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Similarity_measure \"Similarity measure\") two sets are. The scheme was published by [Andrei Broder](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrei_Broder \"Andrei Broder\") in a 1997 conference,[1] and initially used in the [AltaVista](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AltaVista \"AltaVista\") search engine to detect duplicate web pages and eliminate them from search results.[2] It has also been applied in large-scale [clustering](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cluster_analysis \"Cluster analysis\") problems, such as [clustering documents](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Document_clustering \"Document clustering\") by the similarity of their sets of words.[1]\n\n## Jaccard similarity and minimum hash values\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=MinHash&action=edit&section=1 \"Edit section: Jaccard similarity and minimum hash values\")]\n\nThe [Jaccard similarity coefficient](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaccard_index \"Jaccard index\") is a commonly used indicator of the similarity between two sets. Let _U_ be a set and _A_ and _B_ be subsets of _U_ , then the Jaccard index is defined to be the ratio of the number of elements of their [intersection](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intersection_\\(set_theory\\) \"Intersection \\(set theory\\)\") and the number of elements of their [union](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Union_\\(set_theory\\) \"Union \\(set theory\\)\"):\n\n     J ( A , B ) = | A \u2229 B | | A \u222a B | . {\\displaystyle J(A,B)={{|A\\cap B|} \\over {|A\\cup B|}}.}\n\nThis value is 0 when the two sets are [disjoint](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disjoint_sets \"Disjoint sets\"), 1 when they are equal, and strictly between 0 and 1 otherwise. Two sets are more similar (i.e. have relatively more members in common) when their Jaccard index is closer to 1. The goal of MinHash is to estimate _J_(_A_ ,_B_) quickly, without explicitly computing the intersection and union.\n\nLet _h_ be a [hash function](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hash_function \"Hash function\") that maps the members of _U_ to distinct integers, let _perm_ be a random [permutation](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permutation \"Permutation\") of the elements of the set _U_ , and for any subset _S_ of _U_ define _h_ min(_S_) to be the minimal member of _S_ with respect to _h_ \u2218 _perm_ \u2014that is, the member _x_ of _S_ with the minimum value of _h_(_perm_(_x_)). (In cases where the hash function used is assumed to have pseudo-random properties, the random permutation would not be used.)\n\nNow, applying _h_ min to both _A_ and _B_ , and assuming no hash collisions, we see that the values are equal (_h_ min(_A_) = _h_ min(_B_)) if and only if among all elements of  A \u222a B {\\displaystyle A\\cup B} , the element with the minimum hash value lies in the intersection  A \u2229 B {\\displaystyle A\\cap B} . The probability of this being true is exactly the Jaccard index, therefore:\n\n     Pr [ h min ( A ) = h min ( B ) ] = J ( A , B ) , {\\displaystyle {\\text{Pr}}[h_{\\text{min}}(A)=h_{\\text{min}}(B)]=J(A,B),}\n\nThat is, the [probability](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability \"Probability\") that _h_ min(_A_) = _h_ min(_B_) is true is equal to the similarity _J_(_A_ ,_B_), assuming drawing _perm_ from a uniform distribution. In other words, if _r_ is the [random variable](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random_variable \"Random variable\") that is one when _h_ min(_A_) = _h_ min(_B_) and zero otherwise, then _r_ is an [unbiased estimator](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bias_of_an_estimator \"Bias of an estimator\") of _J_(_A_ ,_B_). _r_ has too high a [variance](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variance \"Variance\") to be a useful estimator for the Jaccard similarity on its own, because  r {\\displaystyle r} is always zero or one. The idea of the MinHash scheme is to reduce this variance by averaging together several variables constructed in the same way.\n\n## Algorithm\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=MinHash&action=edit&section=2 \"Edit section: Algorithm\")]\n\n### Variant with many hash functions\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=MinHash&action=edit&section=3 \"Edit section: Variant with many hash functions\")]\n\nThe simplest version of the minhash scheme uses _k_ different hash functions, where _k_ is a fixed integer parameter, and represents each set _S_ by the _k_ values of _h_ min(_S_) for these _k_ functions.\n\nTo estimate _J_(_A_ ,_B_) using this version of the scheme, let _y_ be the number of hash functions for which _h_ min(_A_) = _h_ min(_B_), and use _y_ /_k_ as the estimate. This estimate is the average of _k_ different 0-1 random variables, each of which is one when _h_ min(_A_) = _h_ min(_B_) and zero otherwise, and each of which is an unbiased estimator of _J_(_A_ ,_B_). Therefore, their average is also an unbiased estimator, and by standard deviation for sums of 0-1 random variables, its expected error is O(1/\u221a _k_).[3]\n\nTherefore, for any constant \u03b5 > 0 there is a constant _k_ = O(1/\u03b52) such that the expected error of the estimate is at most \u03b5. For example, 400 hashes would be required to estimate _J_(_A_ ,_B_) with an expected error less than or equal to .05.\n\n### Variant with a single hash function\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=MinHash&action=edit&section=4 \"Edit section: Variant with a single hash function\")]\n\nIt may be computationally expensive to compute multiple hash functions, but a related version of MinHash scheme avoids this penalty by using only a single hash function and uses it to select multiple values from each set rather than selecting only a single minimum value per hash function. Let _h_ be a hash function, and let _k_ be a fixed integer. If _S_ is any set of _k_ or more values in the domain of _h_ , define _h_(_k_)(_S_) to be the subset of the _k_ members of _S_ that have the smallest values of _h_. This subset _h_(_k_)(_S_) is used as a _signature_ for the set _S_ , and the similarity of any two sets is estimated by comparing their signatures.\n\nSpecifically, let _A_ and _B_ be any two sets. Then _X_ = _h_(_k_)(_h_(_k_)(_A_) \u222a _h_(_k_)(_B_)) = _h_(_k_)(_A_ \u222a _B_) is a set of _k_ elements of _A_ \u222a _B_ , and if _h_ is a random function then any subset of _k_ elements is equally likely to be chosen; that is, _X_ is a [simple random sample](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simple_random_sample \"Simple random sample\") of _A_ \u222a _B_. The subset _Y_ = _X_ \u2229 _h_(_k_)(_A_) \u2229 _h_(_k_)(_B_) is the set of members of _X_ that belong to the intersection _A_ \u2229 _B_. Therefore, |_Y_ |/_k_ is an unbiased estimator of _J_(_A_ ,_B_). The difference between this estimator and the estimator produced by multiple hash functions is that _X_ always has exactly _k_ members, whereas the multiple hash functions may lead to a smaller number of sampled elements due to the possibility that two different hash functions may have the same minima. However, when _k_ is small relative to the sizes of the sets, this difference is negligible.\n\nBy standard [Chernoff bounds](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chernoff_bound \"Chernoff bound\") for sampling without replacement, this estimator has expected error O(1/\u221a _k_), matching the performance of the multiple-hash-function scheme.\n\n### Time analysis\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=MinHash&action=edit&section=5 \"Edit section: Time analysis\")]\n\nThe estimator |_Y_ |/_k_ can be computed in time O(_k_) from the two signatures of the given sets, in either variant of the scheme. Therefore, when \u03b5 and _k_ are constants, the time to compute the estimated similarity from the signatures is also constant. The signature of each set can be computed in [linear time](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_time \"Linear time\") on the size of the set, so when many pairwise similarities need to be estimated this method can lead to a substantial savings in running time compared to doing a full comparison of the members of each set. Specifically, for set size _n_ the many hash variant takes O(_n_ _k_) time. The single hash variant is generally faster, requiring O(_n_) time to maintain the queue of minimum hash values assuming _n_ >> _k_.[1]\n\n## Incorporating weights\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=MinHash&action=edit&section=6 \"Edit section: Incorporating weights\")]\n\nA variety of techniques to introduce weights into the computation of MinHashes have been developed. The simplest extends it to integer weights.[4] Extend our hash function h to accept both a set member and an integer, then generate multiple hashes for each item, according to its weight. If item i occurs n times, generate hashes  h ( i , 1 ) , h ( i , 2 ) , \u2026 , h ( i , n ) {\\displaystyle h(i,1),h(i,2),\\ldots ,h(i,n)} . Run the original algorithm on this expanded set of hashes. Doing so yields the [weighted Jaccard Index](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaccard_index#Weighted_Jaccard_similarity_and_distance \"Jaccard index\") as the collision probability.\n\n     J W ( x , y ) = \u2211 i min ( x i , y i ) \u2211 i max ( x i , y i ) {\\displaystyle J_{\\mathcal {W}}(x,y)={\\frac {\\sum _{i}\\min(x_{i},y_{i})}{\\sum _{i}\\max(x_{i},y_{i})}}}\n\nFurther extensions that achieve this collision probability on real weights with better runtime have been developed, one for dense data,[5] and another for sparse data.[6]\n\nAnother family of extensions use exponentially distributed hashes. A uniformly random hash between 0 and 1 can be converted to follow an exponential distribution by [CDF inversion](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inverse_transform_sampling \"Inverse transform sampling\"). This method exploits the many beautiful properties of the [minimum of a set of exponential variables](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponential_distribution#Distribution_of_the_minimum_of_exponential_random"}], "is_error": false, "command": "export SKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MinHash\" --max-chars 15000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "6dac5dae1f18", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/.oh_sessions/e682df0c521948fe87c1cc7c21e0fb6c/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}

{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "URL: https://vldb.org/pvldb/vol14/p2244-ertl.pdf\nType: PDF\nLength: 78998 chars (truncated)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nSetSketch: Filling the Gap between MinHash and HyperLogLog\nOtmar Ertl\nDynatrace Research\nLinz, Austria\notmar.ertl@dynatrace.com\nABSTRACT\nMinHash and HyperLogLog are sketching algorithms that have\nbecome indispensable for set summaries in big data applications.\nWhile HyperLogLog allows counting different elements with very\nlittle space, MinHash is suitable for the fast comparison of sets as it\nallows estimating the Jaccard similarity and other joint quantities.\nThis work presents a new data structure called SetSketch that is\nable to continuously fill the gap between both use cases. Its commu-\ntative and idempotent insert operation and its mergeable state make\nit suitable for distributed environments. Fast, robust, and easy-to-\nimplement estimators for cardinality and joint quantities, as well\nas the ability to use SetSketch for similarity search, enable versatile\napplications. The presented joint estimator can also be applied to\nother data structures such as MinHash, HyperLogLog, or Hyper-\nMinHash, where it even performs better than the corresponding\nstate-of-the-art estimators in many cases.\nPVLDB Reference Format:\nOtmar Ertl. SetSketch: Filling the Gap between MinHash and HyperLogLog.\nPVLDB, 14(11): 2244 - 2257, 2021.\ndoi:10.14778/3476249.3476276\nPVLDB Artifact Availability:\nThe source code, data, and/or other artifacts have been made available at\nhttps://github.com/dynatrace-research/set-sketch-paper.\n1\nINTRODUCTION\nData sketches [16] that are able to represent sets of arbitrary size\nusing only a small, fixed amount of memory have become important\nand widely used tools in big data applications. Although individual\nelements can no longer be accessed after insertion, they are still\nable to give approximate answers when querying the cardinality or\njoint quantities which may include the intersection size, union size,\nsize of set differences, inclusion coefficients, or similarity measures\nlike the Jaccard and the cosine similarity.\nNumerous such algorithms with different characteristics have\nbeen developed and published over the last two decades. They can\nbe classified based on following properties [53] and use cases:\nIdempotency: Insertions of values that have already been added\nshould not further change the state of the data structure. Even\nthough this seems to be an obvious requirement, it is not satisfied\nby many sketches for sets [11, 47, 58, 67, 69].\nThis work is licensed under the Creative Commons BY-NC-ND 4.0 International\nLicense. Visit https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/ to view a copy of\nthis license. For any use beyond those covered by this license, obtain permission by\nemailing info@vldb.org. Copyright is held by the owner/author(s). Publication rights\nlicensed to the VLDB Endowment.\nProceedings of the VLDB Endowment, Vol. 14, No. 11 ISSN 2150-8097.\ndoi:10.14778/3476249.3476276\nCommutativity: The order of insert operations should not be rele-\nvant for the final state. If the processing order cannot be guaranteed,\ncommutativity is needed to get reproducible results. Many data\nstructures are not commutative [13, 32, 65].\nMergeability: In large-scale applications with distributed data\nsources it is essential that the data structure supports the union set\noperation. It allows combining data sketches resulting from partial\ndata streams to get an overall result. Ideally, the union operation\nis idempotent, associative, and commutative to get reproducible\nresults. Some data structures trade mergeability for better space\nefficiency [11, 13, 65].\nSpace efficiency: A small memory footprint is the key purpose of\ndata sketches. They generally allow to trade space for estimation\naccuracy, since variance is typically inversely proportional to the\nsketch size. Better space efficiencies can sometimes also be achieved\nat the expense of recording speed, e.g. by compression [21, 36, 62].\nRecording speed: Fast update times are crucial for many appli-\ncations. They vary over many orders of magnitude for different\ndata structures. For example, they may be constant like for the\nHyperLogLog (HLL) sketch [30] or proportional to the sketch size\nlike for minwise hashing (MinHash) [6].\nCardinality estimation: An important use case is the estimation\nof the number of elements in a set. Sketches have different efficien-\ncies in encoding cardinality information. Apart from estimation\nerror, robustness, speed, and simplicity of the estimation algorithm\nare important for practical use. Many algorithms rely on empirical\nobservations [34] or need to combine different estimators for dif-\nferent cardinality ranges, as is the case for the original estimators\nof HLL [30] and HyperMinHash [72].\nJoint estimation: Knowing the relationship among sets is impor-\ntant for many applications. Estimating the overlap of both sets\nin addition to their cardinalities eventually allows the computa-\ntion of joint quantities such as Jaccard similarity, cosine similarity,\ninclusion coefficients, intersection size, or difference sizes. Data\nstructures store different amounts of joint information. In that re-\ngard, for example, MinHash contains more information than HLL.\nExtracting joint quantities is more complex compared to cardinali-\nties, because it involves the state of two sketches and requires the\nestimation of three unknowns in the general case, which makes\nfinding an efficient, robust, fast, and easy-to-implement estimation\nalgorithm challenging. If a sketch supports cardinality estimation\nand is mergeable, the joint estimation problem can be solved using\nthe inclusion-exclusion principle |\ud835\udc48\u2229\ud835\udc49| = |\ud835\udc48| + |\ud835\udc49| \u2212|\ud835\udc48\u222a\ud835\udc49|.\nHowever, this naive approach is inefficient as it does not use all\ninformation available.\nLocality sensitivity: A further use case is similarity search. If\nthe same components of two different sketches are equal with a\nprobability that is a monotonic function of some similarity measure,\nit can be used for locality-sensitive hashing (LSH) [2, 35, 43, 73], an\n2244\n\nindexing technique that allows querying similar sets in sublinear\ntime. For example, the components of MinHash have a collision\nprobability that is equal to the Jaccard similarity.\nThe optimal choice of a sketch for sets depends on the applica-\ntion and is basically a trade-off between estimation accuracy, speed,\nand memory efficiency. Among the most widely used sketches are\nMinHash [6] and HLL [30]. Both are idempotent, commutative, and\nmergeable, which is probably one of the reasons for their popu-\nlarity. The fast recording speed, simplicity, and memory-efficiency\nhave made HLL the state-of-the-art algorithm for cardinality esti-\nmation. In contrast, MinHash is significantly slower and is less\nmemory-efficient with respect to cardinality estimation, but is\nlocality-sensitive and allows easy and more accurate estimation of\njoint quantities.\n1.1\nMotivation and Contributions\nThe motivation to find a data structure that supports more accurate\njoint estimation and locality sensitivity like MinHash, while having\na better space-efficiency and a faster recording speed that are both\ncloser to those of HLL, has led us to a new data structure called\nSetSketch. It fills the gap between HLL and MinHash and can be\nseen as a generalization of both as it is configurable with a continu-\nous parameter between those two special cases. The possibility to\nfine-tune between space-efficiency and joint estimation accuracy\nallows better adaptation to given requirements.\nWe present fast, robust, and simple methods for cardinality and\njoint estimation that do not rely on any empirically determined\nconstants and that also give consistent errors over the full cardinal-\nity range. The cardinality estimator matches that of both MinHash\nor HLL in the corresponding limit cases. Together with the derived\ncorrections for small and large cardinalities, which do not require\nany empirical calibration, the estimator can also be applied to HLL\nand generalized HyperLogLog (GHLL) sketches over the entire car-\ndinality range. Since its derivation is much simpler than that in\nthe original paper based on complex analysis [30], this work also\ncontributes to a better understanding of the HLL algorithm.\nThe presented joint estimation method can be also specialized\nand used for other data structures. In particular, we derived a new\nclosed-form estimator for MinHash that dominates the state-of-\nthe-art estimator based on the number of identical components.\nFurthermore, our approach is able to improve joint estimation from\nHLL, GHLL, and HyperMinHash sketches except for very small sets\ncompared to the corresponding state-of-the-art estimators. Given\nthe popularity of all those sketches, specifically of MinHash and\nHLL, these side results could therefore have a major impact in\npractice, as more accurate estimates can be made from existing\nand persisted data structures. We also found that a performance\noptimization actually developed for SetSketch can be applied to\nHLL to speed up recording of large sets.\nThe presented methods have been empirically verified by exten-\nsive simulations. For the sake of reproducibility the corresponding\nsource code including a reference implementation of SetSketch\nis available at https://github.com/dynatrace-research/set-sketch-\npaper. An extended version of this paper with additional appendices\ncontaining mathematical proofs and more experimental results is\nalso available [28].\n1.2\nMinHash\nMinHash [6] maps a set \ud835\udc46to an \ud835\udc5a-dimensional vector (\ud835\udc3e1, . . . , \ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc5a)\nusing\n\ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc56:= min\n\ud835\udc51\u2208\ud835\udc46\u210e\ud835\udc56(\ud835\udc51).\nHere \u210e\ud835\udc56are independent hash functions. The probability, that com-\nponents \ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc48\ud835\udc56and \ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc49\ud835\udc56of two different MinHash sketches for sets \ud835\udc48\nand \ud835\udc49match, equals the Jaccard similarity \ud835\udc3d\n\ud835\udc43(\ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc48\ud835\udc56= \ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc49\ud835\udc56) = |\ud835\udc48\u2229\ud835\udc49|\n|\ud835\udc48\u222a\ud835\udc49| = \ud835\udc3d.\nThis locality sensitivity makes MinHash very suitable for set com-\nparisons and similarity search, because the Jaccard similarity can\nbe directly and quickly estimated from the fraction of equal com-\nponents with a root-mean-square error (RMSE) of\np\n\ud835\udc3d(1 \u2212\ud835\udc3d)/\ud835\udc5a.\nMinHash also allows the estimation of cardinalities [12, 13] and\nother joint quantities [15, 19].\nMeanwhile, many improvements and variants have been pub-\nlished that either improve the recording speed or the memory effi-\nciency. One permutation hashing [40] reduces the costs for adding\na new element from O(\ud835\udc5a) to O(1). However, there is a high prob-\nability of uninitialized components for small sets leading to large\nestimation errors. This can be remedied by applying a finalization\nstep called densification [44, 63, 64] which may be expensive for\nsmall sets [27] and also prevents further aggregations. Alternatively,\nfast similarity sketching [17], SuperMinHash [25], or weighted min-\nwise hashing algorithms like BagMinHash [26] and ProbMinHash\n[27] specialized to unweighted sets can be used instead. Compared\nto one permutation hashing with densification, they are merge-\nable, allow further element insertions, and even give more accurate\nJaccard similarity estimates for small set sizes.\nTo shrink the memory footprint, b-bit minwise hashing [39] can\nbe used to reduce all MinHash components from typically 32 or\n64 bits to only a few bits in a finalization step. Although this loss\nof information must be compensated by increasing the number of\ncomponents\ud835\udc5a, the memory efficiency can be significantly improved\nif precise estimates are needed only for high similarities. However,\nthe need of more components increases the computation time and\nthe sketch cannot be further aggregated or merged after finalization.\nBesides the original application of finding similar websites [33],\nMinHash is nowadays widely used for nearest neighbor search [35],\nassociation-rule mining [14], machine learning [41], metagenomics\n[3, 22, 46, 51], molecular fingerprinting [57], graph embeddings [7],\nor malware detection [50, 60].\n1.3\nHyperLogLog\nThe HyperLogLog (HLL) data structure consists of\ud835\udc5ainteger-valued\nregisters \ud835\udc3e1, \ud835\udc3e2, . . . , \ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc5asimilar to MinHash. While MinHash typi-\ncally uses at least 32 bits per component, HLL only needs 5-bit or\n6-bit registers to count up to billions of distinct elements [30, 34].\nThe state for a given set \ud835\udc46is defined by\n\ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc56:= max\ud835\udc51\u2208\ud835\udc46\u230a1 \u2212log\ud835\udc4f\u210e\ud835\udc56(\ud835\udc51)\u230b\nwith \u210e\ud835\udc56(\ud835\udc51) \u223cUniform(0, 1)\n(1)\nwhere \u210e\ud835\udc56are \ud835\udc5aindependent hash functions. \ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc56= 0 is used for\ninitialization and the representation of empty sets. The original\nHLL uses the base \ud835\udc4f= 2, which makes the logarithm evaluation\nvery cheap. We refer to the generalized HyperLogLog (GHLL) when\nusing any \ud835\udc4f> 1 [12, 53]. Definition (1) leads to a recording time of\n2245\n\nO(\ud835\udc5a). Therefore, to have a constant time complexity, HLL imple-\nmentations usually use stochastic averaging [30] which distributes\nthe incoming data stream over all \ud835\udc5aregisters\n\ud835\udc3e\ud835\udc56:= max\ud835\udc51\u2208\ud835\udc46:\u210e1(\ud835\udc51)=\ud835\udc56\u230a1 \u2212log\ud835\udc4f\u210e2(\ud835\udc51)\u230b,\nwhere \u210e1 and \u210e2 are independent uniform hash functions map-\nping to {1, 2, . . . ,\ud835\udc5a} and the interval (0, 1), respectively. Instead of\nusing two hash functions, implementations typically calculate a\nsingle hash value that is split into two parts. The original cardinal-\nity estimator [30] was inaccurate for small and large cardinalities.\nTherefore, a series of improvements have been proposed [34, 59, 70],\nwhich finally ended in an estimator that does not require empirical\ncalibration [23, 24] and that is already successfully used by the\nRedis in-memory data store.\nHLL is not very suitable for joint estimation or similarity search.\nThe reason is that there is no simple estimator for the Jaccard simi-\nlarity and no closed-form expression for the collision probability\nlike for MinHash. The inclusion-exclusion principle gives signifi-\ncantly worse estimates for joint quantities than MinHash using the\nsame memory footprint [18]. A maximum likelihood (ML) based\nmethod [23, 24] for joint estimation was proposed, that performs\nsignificantly better, but requires solving a three-dimensional opti-\nmization problem, which does not easily translate into a fast and\nrobust algorithm. A computationally less expensive method was\nproposed in [49] for estimating inclusion coefficients. However, it\nrelies on precomputed lookup tables and does not properly account\nfor stochastic averaging, which can lead to large errors for small\nsets.\nThese joint estimation approaches have proven that HLL also\nencodes some joint information. This might be one reason why\nHLL\u2019s asymptotic space efficiency, when considering cardinality\nestimation only, is not optimal [53, 54]. Lossless compression [21,\n36, 62] is able to improve the memory efficiency at the expense of\nrecording speed and is therefore rarely used in practice. Approaches\nusing lossy compression [69] are not recommended as their insert\noperations are not idempotent and commutative.\nNevertheless, due to its simplicity, HLL has become the state-\nof-the-art cardinality estimation algorithm, especially if the data\nis distributed. This is evidenced by the many databases like Redis,\nOracle, Snowflake, Microsoft SQL Server, Google BigQuery, Vertica,\nElasticsearch, Aerospike, or Amazon Redshift that use HLL under\nthe hood to realize approximate dis"}], "is_error": false, "command": "export SKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://vldb.org/pvldb/vol14/p2244-ertl.pdf\" --max-chars 15000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "6dac5dae1f18", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/.oh_sessions/e682df0c521948fe87c1cc7c21e0fb6c/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}

In this paper we address the\nfollowing question:\nIf each set S is summarized in a data structure D(S) of n\nbits, how precise an estimate of J(S1, S2) is it possible to\nmake based on D(S1) and D(S2)?\nOur main \ufb01nding is that existing solutions, while highly ef-\n\ufb01cient in general, are not optimal when J is close to 1. We\npresent a novel solution, the Odd Sketch, that yields im-\nproved precision in the high similarity regime.\nAlthough the setting where J is close to 1 has not often\nbeen the primary focus when studying similarity measures,\nthere are many applications where this regime is important.\nConsider a setting where the goal is not just to \ufb01nd a simi-\nlar item, but to provide a short list and ranking of the most\nsimilar items. For example, in the setting of document sim-\nilarity, in a su\ufb03ciently rich environment there may be hun-\ndreds of documents quite similar to another document, and\nthe user wants to see the top ten. For such applications, we\nrequire methods that are very accurate for high similarity\nvalues J.\n1.1\nMinwise Hashing Schemes\nBecause minwise hashing is a building block of our ap-\nproach, and because b-bit minwise hashing is our primary\nalternative for comparison, we review both brie\ufb02y.\n1.1.1\nMinwise Hashing\nMinwise hashing is a powerful algorithmic technique to\nestimate set similarities, originally proposed by Broder et\nal. This estima-\ntor is unbiased, and by independence of the permutations it\ncan be shown that Var[ \u02c6J] = J(1\u2212J)\nk\n.\nObserve that a minhash can be stored as an array of\nlength k containing the minimum for each i = 1, . . . , k. Thus we might ask: Is it possible to\ndo better, avoiding the limits on accuracy that comes when\nsummaries are highly correlated?\n1.2\nOur contribution\nIn this paper, we introduce the Odd Sketch, a compact\nbinary sketch for estimating the Jaccard similarity of two\n\nsets. This binary sketch is similar to a Bloom \ufb01lter with one\nhash function, constructed on the original minhashes with\nthe\u201codd\u201dfeature that the usual disjunction is replaced by an\nexclusive-or operation. That is, we hash each element of the\nminhash into a bit-array data structure. (We will refer to the\nhash function used for this as the \u201csketch hash function\u201d.)\nNow, instead of setting a bit to 1, we \ufb02ip a bit according to\nthe sketch hash value of each element in the minhash. We\napply the Odd Sketches to minhashes, which means that\nthe Odd Sketch records for each hash value whether it is\nmapped to by an odd number of elements in the minhash.\nFigure 2 shows a high level illustration of the construction\nof Odd Sketches.\nA key feature of the Odd Sketch is that when we com-\npute the exclusive-or of two Odd Sketches, the result will\nbe equal to the Odd Sketch of the symmetric di\ufb00erence of\nthe minhashes, i.e., the set of elements in one minhash but\nnot the other. This is because the contribution of all iden-\ntical elements in the minhashes cancel out.\nIn turn, this\nmeans that we are able to base Odd Sketches of size n on\nminhashes of size signi\ufb01cantly above n whenever J is close\nto 1 and there are many identical values in the minhashes,\nso that the variance induced by the minhash step is reduced.\nThe technical di\ufb03culty is to provide a good estimator for\nthe size of a set based on its Odd Sketch. {"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "URL: https://www.inference.org.uk/mackay/dfountain/LT.pdf\nType: PDF\nLength: 45846 chars (truncated)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nLT Codes\nMichael Luby\nDigital Fountain, Inc.\nluby@digitalfountain.com\nAbstract\nWe introduce LT codes, the \ufb01rst rateless erasure codes\nthat are very ef\ufb01cient as the data length grows.\nKeywords: erasure codes, rateless codes, universal codes,\nreliable transport, balls and bins, randomized algorithms\n1. Introduction\nLT codes are the \ufb01rst realization of a class of erasure\ncodes that we call universal erasure codes.\nThe symbol\nlength for the codes can be arbitrary, from one-bit binary\nsymbols to general \u2113-bit symbols. We analyze the run time\nof the encoder and decoder in terms of symbol operations,\nwhere a symbol operation is either an exclusive-or of one\nsymbol into another or a copy of one symbol to another. If\nthe original data consists of k input symbols then each en-\ncoding symbol can be generated, independently of all other\nencoding symbols, on average by O(ln(k/\u03b4)) symbol op-\nerations, and the k original input symbols can be recovered\nfrom any k+O(\n\u221a\nk ln2(k/\u03b4)) of the encoding symbols with\nprobability 1 \u2212\u03b4 by on average O(k \u00b7 ln(k/\u03b4)) symbol op-\nerations.\nLT codes are rateless, i.e., the number of encoding sym-\nbols that can be generated from the data is potentially lim-\nitless. Furthermore, encoding symbols can be generated on\nthe \ufb02y, as few or as many as needed. Also, the decoder can\nrecover an exact copy of the data from any set of the gen-\nerated encoding symbols that in aggregate are only slightly\nlonger in length than the data. Thus, no matter what the\nloss model is on the erasure channel, encoding symbols can\nbe generated as needed and sent over the erasure channel\nuntil a suf\ufb01cient number have arrived at the decoder in or-\nder to recover the data. Since the decoder can recover the\ndata from nearly the minimal number of encoding symbols\npossible, this implies that LT codes are near optimal with\nrespect to any erasure channel. Furthermore, the encoding\nand decoding times are asymptotically very ef\ufb01cient as a\nfunction of the data length. Thus, LT codes are universal\nin the sense that they are simultaneously near optimal for\nevery erasure channel and they are very ef\ufb01cient as the data\nlength grows.\nThe analysis of LT codes is quite different than the anal-\nysis of Tornado codes [16] [17] [15]. In particular, the Tor-\nnado codes analysis is only applicable to graphs with con-\nstant maximum degree, and LT codes use graphs of logarith-\nmic density, and thus the Tornado codes analysis does not\napply. Furthermore, the Tornado codes analysis relies on\ntechniques that lead to a reception overhead that is inher-\nently at least a constant fraction of the data length, whereas\nthe analysis of LT codes shows that the reception overhead\nis an asymptotically vanishing fraction of the data length,\nalbeit at the cost of slightly higher asymptotic encoding and\ndecoding times.\nThe key to the design and analysis of the LT codes is the\nintroduction and analysis of the LT process. This process\nand its analysis is a novel generalization of the classical pro-\ncess and analysis of throwing balls randomly into bins and\nit may be of independent interest. We provide a full analy-\nsis of the behavior of the LT process using \ufb01rst principles of\nprobability theory, which precisely captures the behavior of\nthe data recovery process.\nThe \u201cdigital fountain approach\u201d concept introduced in\n[7] [6] is similar to that of a universal erasure code, and LT\ncodes are the \ufb01rst full realization of this concept. This pa-\nper describes some of the theoretical underpinnings of some\nportions of the work described in [12] [13].\n1.1\nSome encoding details\nThe process of generating an encoding symbol is con-\nceptually very easy to describe:\n\u2022 Randomly choose the degree d of the encoding symbol\nfrom a degree distribution. The design and analysis of\na good degree distribution is a primary focus of the\nremainder of this paper.\n\u2022 Choose uniformly at random d distinct input symbols\nas neighbors of the encoding symbol.\n\u2022 The value of the encoding symbol is the exclusive-or\nof the d neighbors.\n\nWhen using the encoding symbols to recover the original\ninput symbols of the data, the decoder needs to know the de-\ngree and set of neighbors of each encoding symbol. There\nare many different ways of communicating this information\nto the decoder, depending on the application. For exam-\nple, the degree and a list of neighbor indices may be given\nexplicitly to the decoder for each encoding symbol. As an-\nother example, the degree and neighboring indices of each\nencoding symbol may be computed by the decoder implic-\nitly based for example on the timing of the reception of the\nencoding symbol or the position of the encoding symbol rel-\native to the positions of other encoding symbols. As another\nexample, a key may be associated with each encoding sym-\nbol and then both the encoder and decoder apply the same\nfunction to the key to produce the degree and set of neigh-\nbors of the encoding symbol. In this case, the encoder may\nrandomly choose each key it uses to generate an encoding\nsymbol and keys may be passed to the decoder along with\nthe encoding symbols. Each key may instead be produced\nfor example by a deterministic process, e.g., each key may\nbe one larger than the previous key. The encoder and de-\ncoder may have access to the same set of random bits, and\nthe key may be used as the seed to a pseudo-random gener-\nator that uses these random bits to produce the degree and\nthe neighbors of the encoding symbol. There are a variety\nof ways to associate a degree and a set of neighbors with an\nencoding symbol depending on the application, and these\nimplementation details are beyond the scope of this paper.\n1.2\nComparison to traditional erasure codes\nTraditional erasure codes are typically block codes with\na \ufb01xed rate, i.e., k input symbols are used to generate\nn \u2212k redundant symbols for a total of n encoding sym-\nbols, and the rate of the code is k/n. For example, research\nin networking has suggested using implementations of both\nReed-Solomon and Tornado codes for reliable data distri-\nbution applications, and in these cases both k and n either\nare limited to fairly small values due to practical consider-\nations or are \ufb01xed before the encoding process begins, see\ne.g. [21] [26] [2] [7] [6]. Ideally, as is the case with a Reed-\nSolomon code [25], any k of the n encoding symbols is\nsuf\ufb01cient to recover the original k input symbols (see for\nexample [18]). Sometimes, as is the case with a Tornado\ncode [16] [17], slightly more than k of the n encoding sym-\nbols are needed to recover the original k input symbols.\nReed-Solomon codes in practice are only ef\ufb01cient for\nrelative small settings of k and n. The reason is that for a\nstandard implementation of Reed-Solomon codes [26] [2],\nk(n \u2212k)A/2 symbol operations are needed to produce the\nn encoding symbols, where A is the size of the \ufb01nite \ufb01eld\nused. Decoding times are similar. Although there are the-\noretical algorithms for implementing Reed-Solomon codes\nthat are asymptotically faster for both encoding and decod-\ning, i.e., k times polylogarithmic in n, the quadratic time\nimplementations are faster in practice for values of k and n\nof interest.\nTornado codes are block erasure codes that have linear in\nn encoding and decoding times [16] [17] [7] [6]. LT codes\nare somewhat similar to Tornado codes. For example, they\nuse a similar rule to recover the data, and the degree dis-\ntributions used for Tornado codes is super\ufb01cially similar to\nthe degree distributions used for LT codes. However, the ac-\ntual degree distribution used for Tornado codes turns out to\nbe inapplicable to LT codes. For Tornado codes the degree\ndistribution on input symbols is similar to the Soliton dis-\ntribution described later and the degree distribution on the\n\ufb01rst layer of redundant symbols is close to the Poisson dis-\ntribution. The Soliton distribution on input symbols is inap-\nplicable for LT codes, as this distribution cannot be the re-\nsulting degree distribution on input symbols when encoding\nsymbols are generated independently, no matter what the\ndistribution on neighbors of encoding symbols is chosen to\nbe. One could imagine \ufb02ipping the distributions on Tornado\ncodes so that, like the LT codes, the Poisson distribution\nis on the input symbols and the Soliton distribution is on\nthe redundant symbols. However, the distribution induced\non the redundant symbols by the missing input symbols in\nthe Tornado code graph is far from the Soliton distribution,\nand applying the recovery rule to the induced distributions\nclearly won\u2019t lead to a reasonable reception overhead.\nBeyond the differences between LT codes and Tornado\ncodes mentioned previously, LT codes have signi\ufb01cant ap-\nplication advantages over Tornado codes. Let c = n/k be\nthe constant stretch factor of the Tornado code design. Once\nk and n have been \ufb01xed the Tornado encoder produces n en-\ncoding symbols, and cannot produce further encoding sym-\nbols on the \ufb02y if the demand arises. In contrast, the encoder\ncan generate as few or as many encoding symbols as needed\non demand.\nTornado codes use a cascading sequence of bipartite\ngraphs between several layers of symbols, where the in-\nput symbols are at the \ufb01rst layer and redundant symbols are\nat each subsequent layer. In practice, this requires either\nprior construction of the exact same graph structure at both\nthe encoder and decoder, or prior construction of the graph\nstructure at the encoder that is then communicated to the\ndecoder. This preprocessing is quite cumbersome in either\ncase, and in particular the graph structure size is propor-\ntional to n = ck. Although the amortized overhead is less-\nened if the same length data is to be encoded and decoded\nrepeatedly, a different graph structure is required for each\ndata length and thus the preprocessing is quite a large draw-\nback if each data is of a different length. In contrast, the\ndegree distributions used by LT codes are easily computed\nbased on the data length, and this is the only preprocessing\n\nneeded prior to invoking either the encoder or the decoder.\nFor the Tornado codes the decoding depends on having\na suf\ufb01cient number of distinct encoding symbols from the\nn encoding symbols. In some transport applications, the to-\ntal number of encoding symbols transmitted from a sender\nis several times the number of encoding symbols than can\nbe reasonably generated by the Tornado encoder. For these\napplications, in some cases a good policy is to randomly se-\nlect an encoding symbol each time a symbol is to be sent.\nIn this case, it is not hard to see that the average number\nof encoding symbols that a receiver needs to receive to ob-\ntain k distinct encoding symbols among the n = ck en-\ncoding symbols is ck ln(c/(c \u22121)). (Recall that at least k\ndistinct encoding symbols are required to recover the data).\nThus, for example, if c = 2 then the client needs to receive\n1.386k encoding symbols on average in order to receive k\ndistinct encoding symbols. To make this more acceptable,\ne.g., reception of 1.05k encoding symbols, requires c > 10.\nThese values for c makes the Tornado codes unattractive for\nthese types of applications because the encoder and decoder\nmemory usage and the decoding time is proportional to c\ntimes the data length. In contrast, for LT codes the encoder\nand decoder memory usage is proportionalto the data length\nand the decoding time depends only on the data length, in-\ndependent of how many encoding symbols are generated\nand sent by the encoder.\nBoth Reed-Solomon and Tornado codes are systematic\ncodes, i.e. all the input symbols that constitute the data are\ndirectly included among the encoding symbols, whereas LT\ncodes are not systematic.\n1.3\nApplications\nNormally when data is to be transmitted on an IP based\nnetwork, it is partitioned into equal length pieces and placed\ninto packets. Any transmission protocol that uses this type\nof protocol must be able to ensure the reception of all pack-\nets by receivers. This is why for example TCP receiver ac-\nknowledges each received packet and a TCP sender retrans-\nmits unacknowledged packets. Because of these properties,\nTCP/IP does not scale well to transmission over networks\nwith high latencies, to transmission over networks with high\nloss rates, or to highly concurrent transmission of the same\ncontent to multiple clients.\nErasure codes have been proposed to provide reliabil-\nity for a variety of data delivery applications. For one-to-\none delivery, there are advantages in designing the reliabil-\nity mechanism independently from the \ufb02ow and congestion\ncontrol mechanism, as outlined in [1]. The advantage of us-\ning erasure codes in one-to-one data delivery is that it makes\nit easier to design the \ufb02ow and congestion control mecha-\nnisms independently of reliability.\nFor the one-to-many data delivery problem, where scal-\nability of the sender and the network is of paramount im-\nportance, feedback to the sender needs to be limited and\nsending packets that are redundant for some receivers is\nwasteful, reliability may be able to be provided using era-\nsure codes as described in [21], [28], [26], [27], [22], [23],\n[9] [7] [6]. The attractiveness of this approach is that if the\nerasure codes are powerful enough then a single sender can\npotentially be used to reliably deliver data ef\ufb01ciently to a\nlarge number of concurrent receivers without feedback.\nThe one-to-many problem is even more dif\ufb01cult when\ndifferent receivers have different bandwidth connections to\nthe sender and it is desired that each receiver obtain the data\nat the fastest possible speed independent of other receivers.\nReceiver-driven congestion control protocols have been de-\nsigned independently of a reliability protocol that scalably\ndelivers a different fraction of the packets generated to each\nreceiver depending on current network conditions and the\nreceivers bandwidth connection to the sender [19] [30] [4]\n[14]. Erasure codes can be used in conjunction with these\ncongestion control protocols to help provide a complete re-\nliable delivery transport.\nFor many-to-one delivery it is useful for receivers to be\nable to concurrently receive packets for the same data from\nmultiple senders potentially at different locations. In this\ncase receiving redundant packets is a concern, and erasure\ncodes can be used to minimize delivery of redundant pack-\nets. This application is considered in [5], and a partial solu-\ntion is given based on Tornado codes [16] [17].\nLT codes offers compelling advantages for all these dif-\nferent types of data delivery applications that no previous\nerasure codes provides. Using LT codes, close to the mini-\nmal number of encoding symbols can be generated and sent\nin packets to receivers, and the number of packets each re-\nceiver needs before it is able to recover the original data is\nasymptotically close to the minimal possible. When tradi-\ntional erasure codes are used in these solutions, the num-\nber of packets to generate is \ufb01xed based on a best guess\nof network conditions prio"}], "is_error": false, "command": "export SKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://www.inference.org.uk/mackay/dfountain/LT.pdf\" --max-chars 15000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "6dac5dae1f18", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/.oh_sessions/e682df0c521948fe87c1cc7c21e0fb6c/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}

How well can we do? To frame the question\nappropriately,\nit\nis necessary to\ndefine\nthe\n\u201cgoodness\u201d\nof a\nrepresentation of a source. This is accomplished by defining a distortion\nmeasure which is a measure of distance between the random variable\nand its representation. The basic problem in rate distortion theory can\nthen be stated as follows: given a source distribution\nand a distortion\nmeasure, what is the minimum\nexpected distortion achievable at a\nparticular rate? Or, equivalently, what is the minimum rate description\nrequired to achieve a particular distortion?\nOne of the most intriguing\naspects of this theory is that joint\ndescriptions are more efficient than individual descriptions. It is simpler\nto describe an elephant and a chicken with one description than to\ndescribe each alone. This is true even for independent random variables.\nIt is simpler to describe X1 and X2 together (at a given distortion for\neach) than to describe each by itself. Why don\u2019t independent problems\nhave independent solutions? The answer is found in the geometry.\nApparently rectangular grid points (arising from independent descrip-\ntions) do not fill up the space efficiently.\nRate distortion theory can be applied to both discrete and continuous\nrandom variables. The zero-error data compression theory of Chapter 5\nis an important\nspecial case of rate distortion theory applied to a\ndiscrete source with zero distortion.\nWe will begin by considering the simple problem of representing a\nsingle continuous random variable by a finite number of bits.\n336\nElements of Information Theory\nThomas M. The\nquestion is then to find the best possible representation for any given\ndata rate.\nWe first consider the problem of representing a single sample from\nthe source. But it is no longer\nimmediately\nobvious what the representation\nregions and the recon-\nstruction points should be.\nWe can however state two simple properties of optimal regions and\nreconstruction points for the quantization of a single random variable:\nl Given a set of reconstruction points, the distortion is minimized by\nmapping a source random variable X to the representation X(w)\nthat is closest to it. The set of regions of %\u2019 defined by this mapping\nis called a Voronoi or Dirichlet partition defined by the reconstruc-\ntion points.\nl The reconstruction points should minimize the conditional expected\ndistortion over their respective assignment regions.\nThese two properties enable us to construct a simple algorithm to find\na \u201cgood\u201d quantizer: we start with a set of reconstruction points, find the\noptimal set of reconstruction regions (which are the nearest neighbor\nregions with respect to the distortion measure), then find the optimal\nreconstruction points for these regions (the centroids of these regions if\nthe distortion is squared error), and then repeat the iteration for this\nnew set of reconstruction points. The expected distortion is decreased at\neach stage in the algorithm, so the algorithm will converge to a local\nminimum of the distortion. This algorithm is called the Lloyd algorithm\n[ 1811 (for real-valued random variables) or the generaked\nLloyd aZ-\ngorithm [80] (for vector-valued random variables) and is frequently used\nto design quantization\nsystems.\nInstead of quantizing a single random variable, let us assume that we\nare given a set of n i.i.d. random variables drawn according to a\nGaussian distribution.\nThese random variables are to be represented\nusing nR bits. {"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "URL: https://arxiv.org/abs/1911.00675\nType: HTML\nLength: 8405 chars\n\n--- Content ---\n\nSkip to main content\n\narXiv is now an independent nonprofit! [Learn more](https://info.arxiv.org/about) \u00d7\n\n[](https://arxiv.org/IgnoreMe) [ ](https://arxiv.org/) [ Search ](https://arxiv.org/search) [Submit](https://arxiv.org/user/create) [Donate](https://info.arxiv.org/about/donate.html) [Log in](https://arxiv.org/login)\n\nSearch arXiv\n\nPress Enter to search * [Advanced search](https://arxiv.org/search/advanced)\n\n# Computer Science > Data Structures and Algorithms\n\n**arXiv:1911.00675** (cs)\n\n[Submitted on 2 Nov 2019 ([v1](https://arxiv.org/abs/1911.00675v1)), last revised 26 Oct 2020 (this version, v3)]\n\n# Title:ProbMinHash -- A Class of Locality-Sensitive Hash Algorithms for the (Probability) Jaccard Similarity\n\nAuthors:[Otmar Ertl](https://arxiv.org/search/cs?searchtype=author&query=Ertl,+O)\n\nView a PDF of the paper titled ProbMinHash -- A Class of Locality-Sensitive Hash Algorithms for the (Probability) Jaccard Similarity, by Otmar Ertl\n\n[View PDF](/pdf/1911.00675)\n\n> Abstract:The probability Jaccard similarity was recently proposed as a natural generalization of the Jaccard similarity to measure the proximity of sets whose elements are associated with relative frequencies or probabilities. In combination with a hash algorithm that maps those weighted sets to compact signatures which allow fast estimation of pairwise similarities, it constitutes a valuable method for big data applications such as near-duplicate detection, nearest neighbor search, or clustering. This paper introduces a class of one-pass locality-sensitive hash algorithms that are orders of magnitude faster than the original approach. The performance gain is achieved by calculating signature components not independently, but collectively. Four different algorithms are proposed based on this idea. Two of them are statistically equivalent to the original approach and can be used as drop-in replacements. The other two may even improve the estimation error by introducing statistical dependence between signature components. Moreover, the presented techniques can be specialized for the conventional Jaccard similarity, resulting in highly efficient algorithms that outperform traditional minwise hashing and that are able to compete with the state of the art.\n\nComments: | to be published in TKDE, source code available at [this https URL](https://github.com/oertl/probminhash)\n---|---\nSubjects: |  Data Structures and Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.\n_Find sources:_ [\"Rate\u2013distortion theory\"](https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&q=%22Rate%E2%80%93distortion+theory%22) \u2013 [news](https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&q=%22Rate%E2%80%93distortion+theory%22+-wikipedia&tbs=ar:1) **\u00b7** [newspapers](https://www.google.com/search?&q=%22Rate%E2%80%93distortion+theory%22&tbs=bkt:s&tbm=bks) **\u00b7** [books](https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&q=%22Rate%E2%80%93distortion+theory%22+-wikipedia) **\u00b7** [scholar](https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Rate%E2%80%93distortion+theory%22) **\u00b7** [JSTOR](https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Rate%E2%80%93distortion+theory%22&acc=on&wc=on) _( March 2012)__([Learn how and when to remove this message](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal \"Help:Maintenance template removal\"))_\n---|---\n\n**Rate\u2013distortion theory** is a major branch of [information theory](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_theory \"Information theory\") which provides the theoretical foundations for [lossy data compression](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lossy_data_compression \"Lossy data compression\"); it addresses the problem of determining the minimal number of bits per symbol, as measured by the rate _R_ , that should be communicated over a channel, so that the source (input signal) can be approximately reconstructed at the receiver (output signal) without exceeding an expected distortion _D_.\n\n## Introduction\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Rate%E2%80%93distortion_theory&action=edit&section=1 \"Edit section: Introduction\")]\n\n[](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Rate_distortion_theory_problem_setup.svg)Rate distortion encoder and decoder. An encoder  f n {\\displaystyle f_{n}} encodes a sequence  X n {\\displaystyle X^{n}} . The encoded sequence  Y n {\\displaystyle Y^{n}} is then fed to a decoder  g n {\\displaystyle g_{n}} which outputs a sequence  X ^ n {\\displaystyle {\\hat {X}}^{n}} . We try to minimize the distortion between the original sequence  X n {\\displaystyle X^{n}} and the reconstructed sequence  X ^ n {\\displaystyle {\\hat {X}}^{n}} .\n\nRate\u2013distortion theory gives an analytical expression for how much compression can be achieved using lossy compression methods. Many of the existing audio, speech, image, and video compression techniques have transforms, quantization, and bit-rate allocation procedures that capitalize on the general shape of rate\u2013distortion functions.\n\nRate\u2013distortion theory was created by [Claude Shannon](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claude_Shannon \"Claude Shannon\") in his foundational work on information theory.\n\nIn rate\u2013distortion theory, the _rate_ is usually understood as the number of [bits](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bit \"Bit\") per data sample to be stored or transmitted. The notion of _distortion_ is a subject of on-going discussion.[1] In the most simple case (which is actually used in most cases), the distortion is defined as the expected value of the square of the difference between input and output signal (i.e., the [mean squared error](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mean_squared_error \"Mean squared error\")). However, since we know that most [lossy compression](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lossy_compression \"Lossy compression\") techniques operate on data that will be perceived by human consumers (listening to [music](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music \"Music\"), watching pictures and video) the distortion measure should preferably be modeled on human [perception](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perception \"Perception\") and perhaps [aesthetics](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aesthetics \"Aesthetics\"): much like the use of [probability](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability \"Probability\") in [lossless compression](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lossless_compression \"Lossless compression\"), distortion measures can ultimately be identified with [loss functions](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loss_function \"Loss function\") as used in Bayesian [estimation](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Estimation_theory \"Estimation theory\") and [decision theory](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decision_theory \"Decision theory\"). In audio compression, perceptual models (and therefore perceptual distortion measures) are relatively well developed and routinely used in compression techniques such as [MP3](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MP3 \"MP3\") or [Vorbis](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vorbis \"Vorbis\"), but are often not easy to include in rate\u2013distortion theory. In image and video compression, the human perception models are less well developed and inclusion is mostly limited to the [JPEG](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JPEG \"JPEG\") and [MPEG](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MPEG \"MPEG\") weighting ([quantization](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantization_\\(signal_processing\\) \"Quantization \\(signal processing\\)\"), [normalization](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normalization_\\(image_processing\\) \"Normalization \\(image processing\\)\")) matrix.\n\n## Distortion functions\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Rate%E2%80%93distortion_theory&action=edit&section=2 \"Edit section: Distortion functions\")]\n\nDistortion functions measure the cost of representing a symbol  x {\\displaystyle x} by an approximated symbol  x ^ {\\displaystyle {\\hat {x}}} . Typical distortion functions are the Hamming distortion and the Squared-error distortion.\n\n### Hamming distortion\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Rate%E2%80%93distortion_theory&action=edit&section=3 \"Edit section: Hamming distortion\")]\n\n     d ( x , x ^ ) = { 0 if  x = x ^ 1 if  x \u2260 x ^ {\\displaystyle d(x,{\\hat {x}})={\\begin{cases}0&{\\text{if }}x={\\hat {x}}\\\\\\1&{\\text{if }}x\\neq {\\hat {x}}\\end{cases}}}\n\n### Squared-error distortion\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Rate%E2%80%93distortion_theory&action=edit&section=4 \"Edit section: Squared-error distortion\")]\n\n     d ( x , x ^ ) = ( x \u2212 x ^ ) 2 {\\displaystyle d(x,{\\hat {x}})=\\left(x-{\\hat {x}}\\right)^{2}}\n\n## Rate\u2013distortion functions\n\n[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Rate%E2%80%93distortion_theory&action=edit&section=5 \"Edit section: Rate\u2013distortion functions\")]\n\nThe functions that relate the rate and distortion are found as the solution of the following minimization problem:\n\n     inf Q Y \u2223 X ( y \u2223 x ) I Q ( Y ; X ) subject to  D Q \u2264 D \u2217 . {\\displaystyle \\inf _{Q_{Y\\mid X}(y\\mid x)}I_{Q}(Y;X){\\text{ subject to }}D_{Q}\\leq D^{*}.}\n\nHere  Q Y \u2223 X ( y \u2223 x ) {\\displaystyle Q_{Y\\mid X}(y\\mid x)} , sometimes called a test channel, is the [conditional](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conditional_probability \"Conditional probability\") [probability density function](//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability_density_function \"Probability den"}], "is_error": false, "command": "export SKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rate%E2%80%93distortion_theory\" --max-chars 15000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "6dac5dae1f18", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/.oh_sessions/e682df0c521948fe87c1cc7c21e0fb6c/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}
